Salah — Prayer

The Ruling on Men's 'Awrah: Hadith Proofs and Limits
Explains the ruling and proof for male 'awrah limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the male 'awrah and identify the hadith proofs used to establish that the navel is not 'awrah and the knee is 'awrah.

Core Idea
The male 'awrah is from below navel to below knee; the navel is not 'awrah but the knee is.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the precise limits of the male 'awrah in the Hanafi school. The author provides explicit hadith narrations and a precautionary reasoning to settle the boundaries. Understanding these proofs is essential for correctly applying the ruling in prayer and daily life.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
The base text states: 'It (the 'awrah) is from under his navel to under his knee.' Identify the exact boundaries: below navel to below knee.
The matn is quoted as 'وهي من تحت سرته إلى تحت ركبته'.
2
Examine the hadith proofs
Three narrations are cited: al-Hakim: 'What is between the navel and the knee is 'awrah'; al-Daraqutni: 'What is under the navel to the knee is 'awrah'; al-Bayhaqi: 'The thigh is 'awrah.' Note that these are ahad hadith, making the ruling wajib, not fard.
The different wordings ('between', 'under...to') all point to the same area.
3
Understand the definition of 'under the navel'
Shaykh Isma'il from al-Barjandi defines it as the line that goes around the body at the level of the navel, equidistant from the navel on all sides.
This ensures the navel itself is excluded.
4
Analyze the knee as 'awrah
The knee is included because it is the junction of two bones, and distinguishing between thigh and shin is difficult, so the prohibited area prevails as a precaution. Also, the hadith includes the entire area between navel and knee.
The Prophet's thigh exposure in Khaybar was unintentional and not in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهي من تحت سرته إلى تحت ركبته أي ما بينهما فالسرة ليست بعورة والركبة عورة فالغاية هنا لم تدخل تحت المغيا لما رواه الحاكم من غير تعقب ما بين السرة والركبة عورة» (۱) ولرواية الدارقطني ما تحت السرة إلى الركبة عورة ) (۱) ولرواية البيهقي «الفخذ عورة» (٢). وأما انكشاف فخذه في زقاق خيبر فلم يكن قصداً ولأن الركبة ملتقى عظمي الساق والفخذ والتمييز بينهما متعذر فاجتمع المحرم والمبيح فغلب المحرم احتياطاً. كذا قالوا، وقد يقال إن هذا يقتضي أن تكون السرة عورة كما هو رواية عن أبي حنيفة فإنه تعارض في لو صلى عرياناً في الظلمة بلا عذر لا تجوز إجماعاً، ولو كان الوجوب الخوف الرؤية لجازت لكن قد يقال : إنما فرض الستر في الصلاة بالإجماع ولا إجماع فيما إذا كان المصلي هو الذي بحيث لو نظر لرأى عورة نفسه لقول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف بعدم الفساد، فالذي ينبغي الكراهة دون الفساد لترك الواجب دون الشرط، وقولهما لا تفسد صلاته لا ينافي الكراهة فكان هذا هو المختار قول المصنف : (وهي من تحت سرته إلى تحت ركبته قال الشيخ اسماعيل عن البرجندي: ما تحت السرة هو ما تحت الخط الذي يمر بالسرة ويدور على محيط بدنه بحيث يكون بعده عن موقعه في جميع جوانبه على السواء .اهـ. وأما الركبة فسيأتي أنها ملتقى عظم الساق والفخذ. وفي حواشي الخير الرملي قال ابن حجر الهيتمي الشافعي: لم أر لأحد من أئمتنا تحديد الركبة. وعرفها في القاموس بأنها مواصل ما بين أطراف الفخذ وأعالي الساق قال : وصريح ما يأتي في الثامن وما بعده أنها من أول المنحدر عن آخر الفخذ إلى أول أعلى الساق وعليه فكأنهم اعتمدوا في ذلك العرف لبعد تقييد الأحكام بحدها اللغوي لقلته جداً إلا أن يقال : أراد بالموصل ما قررناه وهو قريب. ثم رأيت في الصحاح قال : والركبة معروفة فبهين أن المدار فيها على العرف والكلام في الشرع وهو يدل على أن السرة المحرم والمبيح. وقد يجاب عنه بأنه لم يكن محرماً لدليل اقتضاه وهو ما أخرج أحمد في مسنده عن عمير بن اسحق قال : كنت أمشي مع الحسن بن علي في بعض طرق المدينة فلقينا أبو هريرة فقال للحسن : اكشف لي عن بطنك جعلت فداك حتى أقبل حيث رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقبله قال : فكشف عن بطنه فقبل سرته كذا في شرح المنية. وكان محمد بن الفضل يقول : من السرة إلى موضع نبات شعر العانة ليس بعورة لتعامل العمال في إبداء ذلك الموضع عند الاتزار وفي ستره نوع حرج. وهذا القول ضعيف لأن التعامل بخلاف النص لا كذا في السراج. وفي الظهيرية وحكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في يعتبر . لو رأى رجل غيره مكشوف الركبة ينكر عليه برفق ولا ينازعه إن لج، وإن رآه مكشوف الفخذ ينكر عليه بعنف ولا يضر به إن لج وإن رآه مكشوف السوأة أمره بستر العورة وأدبه على ذلك إن لج ا هـ. وهو يفيد أن لكل مسلم التعزير بالضرب فإنه لم يقيده بالقاضي، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في بابه.
His saying: (It is from under his navel to under his knee) that is, what is between them. The navel is not ‘awrah and the knee is ‘awrah, so the limit here does not include the limited. Due to what al-Hakim narrated without criticism: “What is between the navel and the knee is ‘awrah.” And due to al-Daraqutni’s narration: “What is under the navel to the knee is ‘awrah.” And due to al-Bayhaqi’s narration: “The thigh is ‘awrah.” As for his thigh being exposed in the alley of Khaybar, it was not intentional. And because the knee is the junction of the bones of the shin and thigh, and distinguishing between them is difficult, so the prohibited and the permitted came together, so the prohibited prevailed as a precaution. Thus they said. And it may be said that this implies that the navel is ‘awrah, as in a narration from Abu Hanifa, for there is conflict. If one prays naked in darkness without an excuse, it is not valid by consensus. And if the obligation was the fear of being seen, it would be permissible. But it may be said: The obligation of covering in prayer is by consensus, but there is no consensus regarding when the one praying is such that if he looked he would see his own ‘awrah, due to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that there is no invalidation. So what is appropriate is dislike (karahah) without invalidation, because he left an obligation without a condition. And their statement that his prayer is not invalidated does not contradict dislike. So this is the chosen view. The author’s statement: (It is from under his navel to under his knee). Shaykh Isma‘il from al-Barjandi said: What is under the navel is what is under the line that passes through the navel and goes around the circumference of his body such that its distance from its position in all sides is equal. As for the knee, it will come that it is the junction of the shin bone and thigh bone. In the footnotes of al-Khayr al-Ramli, Ibn Hajar al-Haytami al-Shafi‘i said: I have not seen any of our imams define the knee. It is defined in al-Qamus as the joint between the ends of the thigh and the tops of the shin. He said: And what is explicit in the eighth and after is that it is from the beginning of the descent from the end of the thigh to the beginning of the top of the shin. So it seems they relied in that on custom due to the extreme rarity of restricting rulings by its linguistic definition. But it may be said: He intended by ‘joint’ what we have stated, and it is close. Then I saw in al-Sihah he said: The knee is well-known, so the basis is custom, and the speech in the law indicates that the navel is the prohibited and the permissible. And it may be answered that it was not prohibited due to evidence, which is what Ahmad narrated in his Musnad from ‘Umayr ibn Ishaq who said: I was walking with al-Hasan ibn ‘Ali in some streets of Medina when Abu Hurayra met us and said to al-Hasan: Uncover your belly for me, may I be sacrificed for you, that I may kiss where I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) kiss. He said: So he uncovered his belly and he kissed his navel. Thus in Sharh al-Munyah. Muhammad ibn al-Fadl used to say: From the navel to the place where pubic hair grows is not ‘awrah due to the practice of workers in exposing that place when tying the waist wrapper, and covering it is somewhat burdensome. This statement is weak because practice contrary to text is not considered, as in al-Siraj. In al-Zahiriyyah: The ruling of ‘awrah for the knee is lighter than it is for the thigh. If a man sees another with his knee uncovered, he should advise him gently and not dispute if he persists. If he sees him with his thigh uncovered, he should advise him harshly and it does not harm him if he persists. If he sees him with his private parts uncovered, he should order him to cover his ‘awrah and discipline him for it if he persists. This indicates that every Muslim has the right to administer ta‘zir by beating, for he did not restrict it to the qadi. It will come, Allah willing, in its proper place.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling on the 'awrah of men in prayer. It begins by explaining the matn: the male 'awrah is from under the navel to under the knee, meaning the area between them. The navel itself is not 'awrah, but the knee is. The author provides proofs from hadith: al-Hakim reports 'what is between the navel and knee is 'awrah'; al-Daraqutni reports 'what is under the navel to the knee is 'awrah'; al-Bayhaqi reports 'the thigh is 'awrah'. The commentary then addresses the incident in the alley of Khaybar where the Prophet's thigh was exposed, explaining it was unintentional. The reasoning that the knee is the junction of two bones and therefore the prohibited area is precautionary is given. There is a discussion that if the navel is considered 'awrah, as in a narration from Abu Hanifa, it would conflict with the ruling that it is not 'awrah. The text then discusses the case of praying naked in darkness: it is invalid by consensus. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if a person sees his own 'awrah, the prayer is not invalidated, but the preferred view here is that it is makruh (disliked) because an obligation is left, though not a condition. The author then details the definition of 'under the navel' and the knee, noting that the knee is defined by custom. A weak opinion that the area from navel to pubic hair is not 'awrah due to custom is dismissed. Finally, the passage describes the degrees of enforcement for uncovering different parts: gentle advise for the knee, harsh for the thigh, and discipline for the private parts.

Key Terms
عورة (‘awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered during prayer and in front of non-mahram; for men, from navel to knee.
سرة (surah): The navel; considered not part of ‘awrah for men in the relied-upon Hanafi position.
ركبة (rukbah): The knee; considered part of ‘awrah for men according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is true regarding the male 'awrah?
A) The navel is 'awrah and the knee is not.
B) The navel is not 'awrah and the knee is 'awrah.
C) Both navel and knee are 'awrah.
D) Neither navel nor knee is 'awrah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on the male 'awrah is based on explicit hadith: from below navel to below knee, with the navel not 'awrah and the knee 'awrah.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on the Navel and Enforcement Levels
Explains intra-Hanafi dispute on navel and degrees of enforcement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the navel as 'awrah and apply the graded enforcement for uncovering knee, thigh, and private parts.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi position excludes the navel from 'awrah; enforcement varies: gentle for knee, harsh for thigh, discipline for private parts.

This part of the passage delves into a rare narration from Abu Hanifa that the navel is 'awrah, and then discusses how to advise and correct someone who has uncovered different parts of the 'awrah. Understanding these gradations helps a Hanafi apply the school's rulings with wisdom and proportion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on the navel
The author notes that a narration from Abu Hanifa considers the navel 'awrah, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is not. Understand why the majority holds that the navel is not 'awrah.
The hadith of al-Hasan ibn 'Ali kissing the Prophet's navel is cited as evidence that the navel is not 'awrah.
2
Analyze the ruling on praying naked in darkness
If one prays naked in darkness without excuse, it is invalid by consensus. But if he can see his own 'awrah, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the prayer is not invalidated, though the preferred view is that it is makruh (disliked).
The distinction is between obligation and condition: uncovered 'awrah is a missing obligation, not an invalidating condition.
3
Apply the three levels of enforcement
The passage from al-Zahiriyyah gives: for uncovered knee, gentle advice; for uncovered thigh, harsh advice; for uncovered private parts, order to cover and discipline if persists.
This shows the school's practical approach: severity matches the level of 'awrah.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling on the 'awrah of men in prayer. It begins by explaining the matn: the male 'awrah is from under the navel to under the knee, meaning the area between them. The navel itself is not 'awrah, but the knee is. The author provides proofs from hadith: al-Hakim reports 'what is between the navel and knee is 'awrah'; al-Daraqutni reports 'what is under the navel to the knee is 'awrah'; al-Bayhaqi reports 'the thigh is 'awrah'. The commentary then addresses the incident in the alley of Khaybar where the Prophet's thigh was exposed, explaining it was unintentional. The reasoning that the knee is the junction of two bones and therefore the prohibited area is precautionary is given. There is a discussion that if the navel is considered 'awrah, as in a narration from Abu Hanifa, it would conflict with the ruling that it is not 'awrah. The text then discusses the case of praying naked in darkness: it is invalid by consensus. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if a person sees his own 'awrah, the prayer is not invalidated, but the preferred view here is that it is makruh (disliked) because an obligation is left, though not a condition. The author then details the definition of 'under the navel' and the knee, noting that the knee is defined by custom. A weak opinion that the area from navel to pubic hair is not 'awrah due to custom is dismissed. Finally, the passage describes the degrees of enforcement for uncovering different parts: gentle advise for the knee, harsh for the thigh, and discipline for the private parts.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta‘zir): Discretionary punishment; here, the right to beat someone who persists in uncovering private parts.
كراهة (karahah): Dislike; a ruling that something is discouraged but not forbidden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the appropriate action when seeing a Muslim with his knees uncovered in prayer?
A) Harshly tell him to cover up and argue if he persists.
B) Advise him gently after prayer, and do not dispute if he persists.
C) Ignore it because the knee is not 'awrah.
D) Physically force him to cover his knees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has a nuanced position on the navel (not 'awrah) and prescribes graded enforcement for 'awrah violations: gentle for knee, harsh for thigh, discipline for private parts.

The 'Awrah of a Free Woman: Face, Hands, Feet Excepted
Explains the Hanafi ruling on a free woman's 'awrah with Quran, hadith, and intra-madhhab refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a free woman's 'awrah in prayer, cite the Quranic and Sunnah proofs, and identify the dispute over the back of the palm.

Core Idea
A free woman's 'awrah excludes face, palms, and feet – necessity and ease of exposure justify the exception.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains the well-known Hanafi ruling that a free woman's entire body is 'awrah except her face, palms, and feet. He provides evidence from the Quran and the Prophetic practice concerning ihram. The passage also delves into an intra-madhhab difference: does the exception include the back of the palm? The relied-upon view includes both sides, as supported by a hadith on menstruation and the principle of repelling hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
Locate the matn statement: 'the body of a free woman is 'awrah except her face, palms, and feet'. Note that this is the foundational ruling being commented upon.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Examine the primary proof (Quran)
Cite the verse from Surah al-Nur (24:31) and the interpretation of Ibn 'Abbas that 'what appears' refers to the face and palms. Understand that this is a zanni al-dalala proof because the exception is subject to interpretation.
The Hanafi school adopts Ibn 'Abbas's interpretation.
3
Analyze the secondary proof (Sunnah)
Consider the hadith that the Prophet (PBUH) forbade a woman in ihram from wearing gloves and niqab. The logic is: if these parts were 'awrah, covering them would be mandatory, not prohibited. This strengthens the exception for face and hands.
The ihram hadith provides indirect evidence for the ruling.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the back of the palm
The passage notes that some earlier authorities (zahir al-riwaya) considered only the inner palm (batin) as excepted, while the back (zahir) is 'awrah. However, Qadikhan and later authorities like the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefer that both sides are not 'awrah, citing the hadith about a menstruating woman and the principle of hardship.
The relied-upon fatwa is that the entire palm (both sides) up to the wrist is excepted.
5
Consider the cross-madhhab dispute on the feet
The Shafi'i school holds that the feet are 'awrah, while Hanafis include them in the exception. Understand that the Hanafi evidence relies on the broad Quranic exception and the customary need to expose feet.
This dispute has practical consequences: Hanafi women may pray with feet uncovered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبدن الحرة عورة إلا وجهها وكفيها وقدميها لقول تعالى ﴿ولا يبدين زينتهن إلا ما ظهر منها﴾ [النور: ۳۱] قال ابن عباس : وجهها وكفيها وإن كان ابن مسعود فسره بالثياب كما رواه اسماعيل القاضي من حديث ابن عباس مرفوعاً بسند جيد، ولأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى المحرمة عن لبس القفازين والنقاب ولو كانا عورة لما حرم سترهما. ولأن الحاجة تدعوا إلى إبراز الوجه للبيع والشراء وإلى ابراز الكف للأخذ والإعطاء فلم يجعل ذلك عورة. وعبر بالكف دون اليد كما وقع في المحيط للدلالة على أنه مختص بالباطن وأن ظاهر الكف عورة هو ظاهر الرواية. وفي مختلفات قاضيخان ظاهر الكف وباطنه ليسا بعورة إلى الرسغ ورجحة في شرح المنية بما أخرجه أبو داود في المراسيل عن قتادة .. في عاً أن المرأة إذا حاضت لم يصلح أن يرى منها إلا وجهها ويداها إلى المفصل، ولأن الظاهر أن إخراج ا الكف عن كونه عورة معلول بالابتلاء بالإبداء إذ كونه عورة مع هذا الابتلاء موجب للحرج وهو مدفوع
His saying: (And the body of a free woman is ‘awrah except her face and her palms and her feet) due to the saying of the Most High: “And do not reveal their adornment except what appears of it” [al-Nur 24:31]. Ibn ‘Abbas said: Her face and her palms. Even though Ibn Mas‘ud interpreted it as the outer garments, as narrated by Isma‘il al-Qadi from Ibn ‘Abbas in a marfu‘ hadith with a good chain. And because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade a woman in ihram from wearing gloves and the niqab; if they were ‘awrah, he would not have forbidden covering them. And because necessity calls for revealing the face for buying and selling and revealing the palm for taking and giving, so that is not made ‘awrah. And he expressed with ‘palm’ rather than ‘hand,’ as occurs in al-Muhit, to indicate that it is specific to the inner part, and that the back of the palm is ‘awrah is the apparent narration. In the Mukhtalafat of Qadikhan, the back and inner of the palm are not ‘awrah up to the wrist, and he preferred in Sharh al-Munyah what Abu Dawud narrated in al-Marasil from Qatada... that when a woman menstruates, nothing of her should be seen except her face and her hands up to the joint. And because the apparent is that the exclusion of the palm from being ‘awrah is caused by the difficulty of exposure; for if it were ‘awrah despite this difficulty, it would cause hardship, which is repelled.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling regarding the 'awrah of a free woman during prayer. The matn states that the entire body of a free woman is 'awrah except for the face, palms (kaf), and feet. The author provides proofs from the Quran (verse 31 of Surah al-Nur) and the hadith that the Prophet forbade a woman in ihram from wearing gloves and niqab, indicating that those parts are not 'awrah. There is a difference in interpretation of 'what appears' between Ibn Abbas (face and palms) and Ibn Mas'ud (outer garments). The Hanafi school adopts the view of Ibn Abbas. The passage discusses a detail: the term 'kaf' (palm) is used instead of 'yad' (hand) to indicate that it is specifically the inner part (batin) that is not 'awrah, while the back of the palm (zahir) is 'awrah according to the apparent narration. However, Qadikhan in his Mukhtalafat narrates that both sides are not 'awrah up to the wrist, and this is preferred in Sharh al-Munyah with a hadith from Abu Dawud that when a woman menstruates, only her face and hands to the joint (i.e., wrist) may be seen. The passage also mentions that the ease of exposure (ibtila bil ibda') is the reason for excluding these parts. The text ends in the scan with a discussion on the back of the hand; the full argument is that if the back of the hand were 'awrah, it would cause hardship because of frequent need to expose it.

Key Terms
حرة (hurrah): A free woman (not a slave). Her 'awrah limits differ from a slave woman's in the Hanafi school.
كف (kaff): The palm of the hand (both inner and outer sides). In the Hanafi school, the palm is excluded from ‘awrah up to the wrist.
قدم (qadam): The foot; plural 'qadman' in the text. Hanafis consider the feet not part of the ‘awrah for a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which parts of a free woman's body are not considered 'awrah in prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only the face and hands (including the wrists)
B) The face, palms (both sides), and feet
C) The face, hands, and feet
D) The face, palms (inner only), and feet

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a free woman's face, palms (both sides), and feet are not 'awrah, based on Quranic interpretation, Prophetic practice, and the principle of hardship.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Combining Exposure from Multiple Awrah Members in Salah
Determining when multiple body part exposures invalidate salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for combining exposure from multiple awrah limbs to determine if salah is invalidated, and distinguish the correct position (quarter of smallest limb) from the intra-Hanafi alternative (quarter of total exposed area).

Core Idea
Exposure from multiple awrah limbs is combined; if it reaches a quarter of the smallest exposed limb, prayer is invalid.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school handles the exposure of multiple body parts (awrah) during salah. Based on the text of Imam Muhammad in al-Ziyadat, the ruling is that if the total exposure from different limbs equals a quarter of the smallest limb among those exposed, and this exposure lasts for the duration of a complete rukn, the prayer is invalidated. This reflects the school's precautionary approach to the 'awrah. The lesson also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute between the preferred view and that of some later scholars who considered a quarter of the total exposed area.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? Exposure of multiple awrah limbs during salah. What is the ruling? If the combined exposure reaches a quarter of the smallest exposed limb, the prayer is invalid (batil).
This ruling applies to both men and women, but the awrah definition differs.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the text from Imam Muhammad in al-Ziyadat: a woman with exposure on hair, thigh, shin, chest, and thick awrah; if combined equals a quarter of a small limb, prayer invalid.
This is a hadith-like transmission (zanni al-thubut) but authoritative in Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The text explicitly states the quarter is of a 'small limb', indicating the smallest exposed limb, not the total area. This is further supported by analogy with combining scattered najasah.
The phrase 'ربع عضو صغير منها' is key.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some later Hanafis (Ibn al-Humam) argued that all limbs are like one limb, so the quarter should be of the total exposed area. Identify their reasoning and the Hanafi response.
The response: the phrase 'like one limb' refers only to combining, not to the threshold.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the quarter of the smallest exposed limb, as per the clear text of Muhammad. This is the majority view and the one adopted for fatwa.
Also note the condition: exposure must last for a duration equal to a rukn with its sunnah.

لا ربع جميع العورة المنكشفة لا تبطل وحاصله أنه ينظر إلى مجموع المنكشف الأعضاء المنكشفة بعضها وإلى مجموع المنكشف، فإن بلغ مجموع ربع مجموع منع وإلا فلا، وهو ظاهر كلام محمد في الزيادات في موضع آخر حيث قال : إذا صلت وانكشف شيء من شعرها وشيء من ظهرها وشيء من فرجها إن كان بحال لو جمع بلغ الربع منع وإلا فلا. ثم قال الزاهدي ولم يذكر أنه بلغ ربع أصغرها أم أكبرها. وفي شرح المجمع لابن الملك : اعلم أن انكشاف ما دون الربع معفو إذا كان في عضو واحد، وإن كان في عضوين أو أكثر وجمع بلغ ربع أدنى عضو منها يمنع جواز الصلاة ا هـ. وهو تفصيل لا دليل عليه فإن الدليل اقتضى اعتبار الربع سواء كان في عضو واحد أو عضوين. وأطلق في المنع وهو مقيد بما إذا كان في الزمن الكثير لما في فتح القدير الحاصل أن الانكشاف الكثير في الزمن القليل لا يفسد والانكشاف القليل في الزمن الكثير أيضاً لا يفسد، والمفسد الانكشاف الكثير في الزمن الكثير، وقدر الكثير ما يؤدي فيه ركن والقليل دونه، فلو أن الانكشاف ربع أصغر الأعضاء منع اهـ. وفي شرح الشيخ اسمعيل : فتحرر أن المعتبر أدنى عضو انكشف بعضه ربع لا أدنى عضو من أعضائها ولو لم يكشف منه شيء كما توهمه عبارة درر البحار فليتدبر. وإن ما في الفتح من يجمع المتفرق من العورة وشرح الكنز ليس المذهب كما ترى، وعلى المذهب ما في شرح ابن ملك معزياً إلى شرح الزيادات. ثم نقل عبارة ابن الشحنة من قوله وفيه: أي فيما ذكره في الزيادات وما نقله بديع الدين نفي لما ذكره شارح الكنز إلى أن قال : والعجب من شيخنا يعني ابن الهمام كيف تبعه عليه وأقره مع أنه خلاف منصوص محمد. وقولهم إن جميع الأعضاء في كعضو واحد، المراد به في اعتبار الجمع لا في اعتبار ربع مجموعها فتأمله ممعناً فيه النظر، والله تعالى الهادي إلى الصواب اهـ قلت: ونص عبارة الزيادات على ما في القنية انكشف من شعرها شيء فخذها في صلاتها ومن شيء ومن ساقها شيء من ظهرها ومن بطنها، فلو جمع يكون قدر ربع شعرها أو ساقها أو فخذها لم تجزها صلاتها لأن كلها عورة واحدة اهـ. قال في القنية : وهذا نص على أمرين الناس عنهما غافلون : أحدهما أنه لا يعتبر الجمع بالإجزاء كالأسداس والأسباع والاتساع بل بالقدر، والثاني أن المكشوف من الكل لو كان قدر ربع أصغرها من الأعضاء المكشوفة يمنع الجواز حتى لو انكشف الساق تسعها تمنع لأن المكشوف قدر ربع. اهـ. ونقل ابن أمير حاج غبارة القنية ثم قال : إن الأمر الثاني منصوص عليه في المحيط الرضوي نقلاً من الزيادات. قوله: (وقدر الكثير ما يؤدي فيه ركن) أي بسنته كما قيده في المنية. قال شارحها ابن أمير حاج : أي بماله من السنة أي بما هو مشروع فيه من الكمال السني كالتسبيحات في الركوع والسجود مثلاً . وهو تقييد غريب ووجهه قريب، ولم أقف على التقييد بكونه قصيراً أو طويلاً اهـ. أي تقييد الركن أي هل المراد منه قدر ركن طويل بسنته كالقعود الأخير، أو القيام المشتمل على قراءة
So it is permitted that he considered that, so what they said does not refute it. It is amazing because it is not understood from what was said that the whole is one limb, but rather it is a clarification of the thick 'awrah. How? They have explicitly stated that each of the penis and testicles is an independent limb? And it was corrected in al-Hidayah and al-Khaniyyah because each is considered a separate limb in diyah, so here too for precaution, and in one narration that the whole is one limb. In any case, no one said that the front and back are one limb, unless it is said that his intent is that the front with its surroundings is one limb, and the back with its surroundings is one limb. As for the knee with the thigh, the correct view is that they are one limb, as in al-Tanjis, and it is the chosen view, as in al-Khulasa, because the knee is the meeting point of the shin and thigh bones, so it is not an independent limb in reality; it is only made 'awrah as a follower of the thigh for precaution. Thus, if one prays with his knees exposed and the thigh covered, it is permissible. As in al-Munyah. And in its commentary: the correct view is that the ankle is not an independent limb but rather with the shin is one limb, so on this basis, only a quarter of the shin with a quarter of the ankle, or the measure of their quarter, prevents. The anus is one limb, and each buttock is one limb – this is the most correct – and each ear is a separate limb. The woman's breast: if it is prominent, it follows her chest; if it is sagging, it is an independent limb. Prominent means erect from the chest, not relaxed. 'Thady' is used as masculine and feminine, but masculine is more famous; in al-Mughrib only the masculine is mentioned. What is between the navel and pubes is one limb, and what is intended is around the entire body. As in al-Muhit. And in al-Ziyadat: a woman prayed and something of her thigh, something of her shin, something of her chest, and something of her thick 'awrah were exposed. If it were gathered and reached a quarter of a small limb of hers, her prayer is not permissible, because at the time of exposure it is like one limb, so they are combined like scattered najasah in places, and like perfume for a muhrim in places, unlike holes as we mentioned in wiping over the khuffs. And the commentator mentioned that it is appropriate because all limbs are like one limb. And the commentator said, etc. He said in al-Nahr after mentioning the statement of al-Zayla'i and he approved it in Fath al-Qadir and others. He said in 'Uqd al-Fara'id: apparently he understood that the base rule is that what invalidates is a quarter of what is exposed, but this is contrary because the invalidator is only like that when the exposure is in one limb, and there it is considered by parts, as when multiple spots on his thigh are exposed. But in our case, exposure occurred in multiple limbs, each of which is 'awrah. Precaution requires considering the smallest of them because the obstacle exists by it. So one looks at the amount exposed from all of them: if it reaches the exposure of the smallest, it invalidates as a precaution; otherwise, it would necessitate validity of prayer with a quarter of a quarter of 'awrah exposed – and this is the rule transmitted from Muhammad, and this is necessary by considering parts, and no one says it. So if you realize this, it becomes clear that what Ibn al-Malik said agrees with what is in al-Ziyadat, and his statement in al-Bahr that it is a detail without evidence is denied. Badī' al-Din said: What is in al-Ziyadat is a text on two matters that people are heedless of: first, that combination is not restricted to parts like sixths and ninths, but by amount; second, that if the exposed from all is a quarter of the smallest limbs, it prevents, even if half of an eighth of the thigh and half of an eighth of the ear are exposed, and that ear or more – not a quarter of the entire exposed 'awrah – does not invalidate. The summary is that one looks at the total of the exposed limbs partially and at the total exposed: if it reaches a quarter of the total, it prevents; otherwise not. This is apparent from the words of Muhammad in al-Ziyadat in another place where he said: If a woman prayed and something of her hair, something of her back, and something of her private part were exposed, if it were gathered and reached a quarter, it prevents; otherwise not. Then al-Zahidi said: He did not mention whether it reached a quarter of the smallest or the largest. And in Sharh al-Majma' by Ibn al-Malik: Know that exposure of less than a quarter is excused if it is in one limb; if in two or more limbs and combined it reaches a quarter of the lowest limb among them, it prevents the validity of prayer. This is a detail without evidence, because the evidence necessitated considering the quarter whether in one limb or two. And it is unrestricted in preventing, but it is restricted to when it is in much time, according to Fath al-Qadir. The outcome is that much exposure in little time does not invalidate, and little exposure in much time also does not invalidate; what invalidates is much exposure in much time. Much time is defined as that in which a pillar is performed, and little is less. So if the exposure reaches a quarter of the smallest limb, it prevents. And in Sharh al-Shaykh Isma'il: It is established that the consideration is the lowest limb of which a quarter was exposed, not the lowest limb of her limbs from which nothing was exposed, as the phrase of Durar al-Bihar might falsely suggest. So reflect. What is in Fath of combining scattered 'awrah and Sharh al-Kanz is not the madhhab, as you see; the madhhab is what is in Sharh Ibn al-Malik, attributed to Sharh al-Ziyadat. Then he transmitted the statement of Ibn al-Shihnah: 'It (what is in al-Ziyadat and what Badī' al-Din transmitted) negates what the commentator of al-Kanz mentioned, until he said: I am amazed at our teacher (meaning Ibn al-Humam) how he followed him and approved it, even though it contradicts the explicit text of Muhammad.' And their saying that all limbs are like one limb is intended in terms of combination, not in terms of a quarter of their total. Reflect with full consideration. Allah is the Guide to what is correct. I say: The explicit text of al-Ziyadat according to al-Qunyah: 'If something of her hair, something of her thigh, something of her shin, something of her back, and something of her stomach were exposed, if it were gathered and equaled a quarter of her hair or her shin or her thigh, her prayer is not valid because all of it is one 'awrah.' He said in al-Qunyah: This is a text on two matters that people are heedless of: first, that combination is not by parts like sixths, sevenths, and ninths, but by amount; second, that the exposed from all, if it were the measure of a quarter of the smallest of the exposed limbs, prevents validity, such that even if the shin is exposed by a ninth, it prevents because the exposed is a quarter. Ibn Amir Hajj transmitted it from al-Qunyah and then said: The second matter is explicitly stated in al-Muhit al-Radawi, transmitted from al-Ziyadat. His statement: (And much time is what a pillar is performed in) i.e., with its sunnah, as al-Munyah restricted it. The commentator Ibn Amir Hajj said: meaning with what is sunnah for it, i.e., with what is prescribed of sunnah completion, like the tasbihat in ruku' and sujud. This is a strange restriction, but its justification is close. I have not come across the restriction of it being short or long. Meaning the restriction of the pillar: does it mean the length of a long pillar with its sunnah, like the last sitting, or the standing that includes recitation?
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on exposure of multiple 'awrah body parts during salah and how to determine whether the prayer is invalidated. The author addresses the issue of combining exposure from various limbs, as found in the narration of Muhammad in al-Ziyadat. Different opinions are presented regarding whether to consider the total exposure as a quarter of a single 'member' or to look at the smallest exposed limb's quarter. The correct Hanafi position, as derived from Muhammad's texts, is that if the combined exposure from multiple 'awrah limbs reaches a quarter of the smallest limb among those exposed, the salah is invalid. This is analogous to combining scattered najasah (impurity) on the body or multiple applications of perfume for a muhrim. The discussion clarifies that the combination is by absolute amount, not by fractional parts like sixths or ninths. The commentator criticizes some later authorities, such as Ibn al-Humam, for following a contrary view that would consider a quarter of the total exposed area from all limbs, which is not supported by the clear texts of Muhammad. The passage also defines what constitutes a 'limb' for 'awrah purposes, e.g., the knee is part of the thigh, the ankle is part of the shin, and each ear is separate. Additionally, it introduces the condition that the exposure must last for a duration in which a complete pillar (rukn) of the prayer is performed, with its sunnah, for it to invalidate. The overall lesson is that the Hanafi school has a detailed methodology for handling multiple exposures, emphasizing precaution by looking at the smallest limb.

Key Terms
العورة (al-'awrah): The parts of the body that must be covered during prayer; for a free woman, the entire body except face and hands; for a man, from navel to knees.
الجمع (al-jam'): Combining the exposure from multiple body parts to see if total reaches the threshold of a quarter of a limb.
الربع (al-rubu'): A quarter – the threshold for exposure that invalidates prayer. If a quarter of any 'awrah limb is exposed, or combined exposure equals a quarter of the smallest exposed limb, prayer is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a woman prays and during the prayer, 1/8 of her thigh is exposed and 1/8 of her ear is exposed for a duration that includes a rukn with its sunnah, what is the ruling according to the correct Hanafi position?
A) Her prayer is invalid because the total exposure equals 1/4 of the ear, which is the smallest exposed limb.
B) Her prayer is valid because each exposure separately is less than a quarter.
C) Her prayer is invalid because the total exposed area is 1/4 of the sum of all her limbs.
D) Her prayer is valid only if the exposure lasts less than one rukn.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school combines exposure from multiple awrah limbs and invalidates the prayer if the combined amount reaches a quarter of the smallest exposed limb, lasting for the duration of a rukn with its sunnah.

Covering Exposed 'Awrah During Prayer: When Does It Invalidate?
Explains when covering after exposure invalidates prayer based on cause of exposure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on covering exposed 'awrah during prayer and distinguish between cases based on the cause of exposure and performance of a pillar.

Core Idea
If 'awrah is exposed by your own action, covering saves only if no rukn is performed.

This lesson unpacks a subtle ruling from al-Bahr al-Raiq. You already know that covering the 'awrah is a condition for prayer. But what happens if it becomes uncovered mid-prayer and you cover immediately? The answer depends on who caused the exposure—and even on which Hanafi authority you follow. We will walk through the text to clarify the conditions and the intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage and find the two outcomes: (1) if exposure is not by your action, covering immediately saves the prayer; (2) if by your action, the prayer becomes invalid (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) only after you perform a complete pillar (rukn).
2
Distinguish the Cause of Exposure
Note the critical distinction: '...إن لم يكن بفعله... وإن كان بفعله...' The cause determines the ruling. Exposure by external factors (wind, crowding) is treated differently than by your own hand.
3
Pinpoint the Condition of Performing a Pillar
The text says: 'اعتبر أداء الركن حقيقة' – the actual performance of a rukn is considered. So even if exposure was by your own action, if you cover before bowing, prostrating, or standing a pillar’s length, the prayer remains valid (according to Sahibayn).
4
Explore the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Note that this is a 'taqyid gharib' – a strange restriction. Abu Hanifa holds that any intentional exposure invalidates immediately. Compare how the two positions affect the ruling.
5
Apply to Analogous Cases
The text analogizes to standing in the women's row due to crowding or standing on impurity. These also 'prevent' but do not 'invalidate' unless a rukn is performed. Work through the slave woman example to see how the principle applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انكشف فغطاها في الحال لا تفسد إن لم يكن بفعله وإن كان بفعله فسدت في الحال عندهم كذا في القنية وهو تقييد غريب وهذا عند أبي يوسف ومحمد اعتبر أداء الركن حقيقة وعلى هذا الخلاف لو قام في صف النساء للازدحام أو قام على نجاسة مانعة. وإنما بالمنع دون الفساد ليشمل ما إذا أحرم مكشوف العورة فإنه مانع من الانعقاد وما إذا انكشف بعد الإحرام فإنه يمنع صحتها، وحكم النجاسة المانعة كالانكشاف المانع. وتفرع على ما ذكرنا ما في المحيط : أمة صلت بغير قناع فرعفت ثم أعتقت فتوضأت ثم تقنعت وعادت إلى الصلاة جازت لأنها ما أدت شيئاً الصلاة مع كشف العورة، وإن عادت ثم عبر المصنف امع من الكشف .
If it became exposed and he covered it immediately, it does not invalidate if it was not by his action, and if it was by his action, it invalidates immediately according to them—thus in al-Qunyah, and it is a strange restriction. This is because according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the actual performance of a pillar is considered. And upon this difference, if he stood in the row of women due to crowding, or stood upon an impurity that prevents. [The expression] 'by prevention not invalidation' is used to include the case where he begins prayer with exposed ‘awrah, for it prevents the validity of the initiation, and the case where it becomes exposed after initiation, for it prevents the validity of the prayer. The ruling of impure spots that prevent is like the exposure that prevents. And derived from what we have mentioned is what is in al-Muhit: A slave woman prayed without a headcover, then she was freed, then she performed wudu, then she covered and returned to prayer; it is valid because she had not performed any part of prayer with exposed ‘awrah. But if she returned and then the author expressed? from covering?
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling when the ‘awrah becomes uncovered during prayer and the person covers it immediately. The school, based on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, distinguishes whether the exposure was caused by the worshipper's own action or not. If it was not by his action, covering immediately saves the prayer. If it was by his action, the prayer becomes invalid only if he then performs a complete pillar (rukn) such as ruku' or sujud; if he covers before performing any pillar, the prayer remains valid. This is a 'strange restriction' (taqyid gharib) because the typical Hanafi view is that any intentional exposure invalidates immediately. The text also compares this to standing on an impurity that prevents the validity. An example from al-Muhit clarifies: a slave woman who began prayer without a headcover (which is permissible for a slave) is freed during prayer; if she covers before performing a pillar, her prayer is valid.

Key Terms
كشف العورة (kashf al-`awrah): Exposure of the private parts during prayer, which is a condition for validity.
تقيد غريب (taqyid gharib): A strange restriction in al-Qunyah, meaning an unusual or exceptional ruling.
ركن (rukn): Pillar of prayer, such as ruku', sujud, standing; its performance after exposure triggers invalidation in the Sahibayn view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately, regardless of covering
B) Only if he performs a complete pillar after the exposure
C) Only if he fails to cover at all
D) Only if the exposure occurs in the women's row

Key Takeaway
The ruling on covering exposed 'awrah during prayer hinges on whether the exposure was by the worshipper's own action and whether a complete pillar was performed before covering.

The `Awrah of a Female Slave During Prayer
Defines the `awrah of the slave woman and discusses subsidiary issues like emancipation during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the `awrah of a female slave, identify the proof from the practice of `Umar ibn al-Khattab, and apply the ruling to cases of emancipation during prayer.

Core Idea
A slave woman's `awrah in prayer is only from navel to knee, like a man's, based on `Umar's command.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a distinct Hanafi ruling: the `awrah of a female slave during prayer is minimal compared to a free woman. The ruling is supported by a clear action of `Umar ibn al-Khattab and further extended to various subsidiary issues such as partial emancipation and hermaphrodites. Understanding this ruling requires familiarity with the proof, its scope, and how it applies when the slave's status changes mid-prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states the `awrah of a female slave is from navel to knee, like a man. Note that the head, shoulders, and legs are not `awrah.
2
Locate the proof
The ruling is based on the practice of `Umar ibn al-Khattab who struck a slave woman covering her head and commanded her to uncover, narrated by Abd al-Razzaq with a sound chain.
This is a hadith mawquf (companion narration) which is zanni in thubut but qati in dalalah.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'Uncover your head' directly indicates that covering the head is not required for slave women. The prohibition of imitating free women strengthens the ruling.
The illah is to avoid confusion with free women and prevent harassment.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider a mustas'ah (slave earning freedom) as free, requiring full covering, while Abu Hanifa considers her a slave. Understand the practical difference.
The ruling on mustas'ah affects whether she must cover her head during prayer.
5
Apply to mid-prayer emancipation
If a slave is freed during prayer, she must cover before performing a pillar; if she covers with a small action, the prayer is valid; otherwise, it is invalid. Distinguish from the case of a naked person finding clothing.
The key distinction: for the slave, the obligation arose during prayer; for the naked person, it existed before.

His statement: '(The slave woman is like the man, and her back and belly are `awrah) because they are places of desire, unlike [the hair?]. Both the back and belly are places of desire, and whatever is besides these parts of the body is not `awrah, whether head, shoulder, or leg, due to hardship. And Abd al-Razzaq narrated with a sound chain from Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, that he struck a slave woman who was covering her head and said: 'Uncover your head, do not imitate free women.' Then in the clarification of the Malikiyyah: If it is said, 'Why did Umar prevent slave women from imitating free women?' The answer is that foolish men customarily harass slave women, and Umar feared that the matter would become confused so that foolish men would harass free women, and thus the tribulation would be more severe. That is the meaning of His statement, Exalted and Majestic: 'That is more appropriate that they be known and not be harmed' [al-Ahzab: 59], meaning they are distinguished by their signs from others. Its apparent meaning is that it is disliked for a slave woman to cover her entire body, and that is not hidden. In any case, one should say: It is recommended for her to cover in prayer, but I have not seen it from our imams; rather it is transmitted from the Shafi'is as mentioned by al-Nawawi. And 'slave woman' in language is the opposite of free woman, thus in al-Sihah. For this reason, he used the general term to include the qinnah, mudabbara, mukataba, mustas'ah, and umm al-walad. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the mustas'ah is free, and by mustas'ah is meant a partially freed slave. As for a mustas'ah who is pawned, if the pawner frees her while he is insolvent, she is free by agreement. And there occurred hesitation in some lessons concerning the side (jamb): is it `awrah or not? I mentioned that it is `awrah. Then I saw in al-Qunyah that he said: 'The side follows the belly, and the more correct view is that what follows the belly is a follow-up to it.' And if she is freed while in prayer with her head or similar exposed, and she covers it with a small action before performing a pillar, it is valid; not with a large action or after a pillar—thus in many books. And the commentator restricted it by [saying] that she must have performed a pillar after knowing of the emancipation, so he made condition of knowledge following al-Zahiriyyah. And it is clearly stated in al-Mujtaba that if she prayed for a month without a headcover and then learned that she had been freed a month ago, she must repeat them. And in the Fatawa of Qadikhan: If his `awrah became exposed and he performed a pillar with it, the prayer is invalid, whether he knows or not. Many similar rulings are mentioned. These two explicit statements are more appropriate than the contrary implication. And in 'Iddat al-Fatawa: A man died in Mecca, and it became necessary for a woman to repeat a year of prayer. I said: It is a man who attached the freedom of his concubine to his death; he died in Mecca, and she did not know of his death, and she prayed with uncovered head. She must repeat the prayer from the time of his death. And in al-Muhit: Unlike the case of a naked person who finds clothing during prayer, for he must restart because covering was obligatory due to a cause prior to commencing (i.e., uncovered `awrah existed before prayer), so when he is addressed to cover during prayer, it is based on that cause, as if the address preceded the prayer and he omitted it. In contrast to the slave woman, since emancipation is the cause of the address to cover, and it occurs during prayer, and she has covered as soon as she could. And the apparent implication is that if she was unable to cover, and did not cover like a free woman, her prayer is not invalidated. This is stated explicitly in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli, attributed to al-Bada'i'. And in Sharh al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The khuntha (hermaphrodite): if he is a slave, his `awrah is the `awrah of a slave woman; if he is free, we order him to cover his entire body because it is possible that he is a woman. If he covers from navel to knee and prays, some say he must repeat because he might be a woman; others say he does not have to repeat because he might be a man. A good branch case that I have not seen transmitted from our imams, mentioned in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab: If a man said to his slave woman, 'If you pray a valid prayer, you are free,' and she prays with uncovered head: if she was unable to cover, her prayer is valid and she is freed; if she was able to cover, her prayer is valid but she is not freed, because if she...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals extensively with the `awrah of a female slave (amah) during prayer. It begins by stating the rule that her `awrah is limited to the front and back, i.e., from navel to knee, like a man. This is based on the rational basis that her head and limbs are not primary objects of desire, and on the practice of the Rightly-Guided Caliph Umar, who struck a slave woman for covering her head and said 'Uncover your head, do not imitate free women.' This shows that covering the head is not required for a slave. The passage then discusses various categories of slaves (qinnah, mudabbara, mukataba, mustas'ah, umm al-walad) and notes that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, a mustas'ah (a slave earning to free herself) is considered free. There follows a discussion on whether the side of the body (jamb) is `awrah; the correct view is that it follows the belly. The text then addresses the scenario where a slave woman is freed during prayer: she must cover before performing a pillar, and if she does so, the prayer is valid; but if she was unaware of her freedom and later learns, she must repeat prayers performed while uncovered. The comparison with a naked person who finds clothes during prayer is made: the naked person must restart because the obligation of covering existed before prayer, whereas for the slave woman, the obligation arose during prayer and she covered promptly. The passage concludes with a discussion of the `awrah of a khuntha (hermaphrodite): if a slave, his `awrah is like that of a slave woman; if free, he must cover his entire body due to uncertainty. Finally, a branch case from Sharh al-Muhadhdhab about conditional emancipation: if a master says to his slave girl, 'If you pray a valid prayer, you are free,' and she prays with uncovered head, the prayer's validity and her freedom depend on whether she was able to cover her head.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave, as opposed to a free woman. The term includes various categories: qinnah, mudabbara, mukataba, mustas'ah, and umm al-walad.
الجنب (al-janb): The side of the body, between the armpit and hip. The discussion resolves that it is not `awrah independently; it follows the belly.
المستسعاة (al-mustas'ah): A slave who is partially freed and earning to pay off the remaining portion of her value.
الخنثى (al-khuntha): A hermaphrodite person whose gender is ambiguous. The `awrah depends on whether they are a slave or free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the `awrah of a female slave during prayer according to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage?
A) Her entire body except face and hands
B) From navel to knee
C) Her head and feet are not `awrah
D) She must cover everything

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinctively limits the `awrah of a slave woman to navel to knee, based on the practice of `Umar, with significant implications for prayer validity during status changes.

Ruling on Garment with Partial Purity and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling for one who finds a garment with a quarter or less pure, and the dispute between the two shaykhs and Muhammad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying in a partially pure garment and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the choice to pray naked.

Core Idea
A quarter of purity in a garment makes it like the whole; less than a quarter gives a choice (two shaykhs) or no choice (Muhammad).

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario where a person has only one garment, and part of it is impure. We will see that the Hanafi school has a unique rule that a quarter of the garment takes the ruling of the whole in necessity. However, when less than a quarter is pure, a well-known intra-madhhab dispute arises: do you have the right to choose between wearing it or praying naked? We will explore the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios based on the amount of pure area.
The matn gives two cases: quarter pure and less than quarter pure. Understand the ruling for each according to the text.
The quarter is a legal measure unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
Determine the legal principle behind the quarter rule.
The principle is that in necessity, a quarter stands for the whole. This makes the garment effectively pure for the ruling.
This is based on the concept of takhyir in necessity.
3
Understand the options given by the two shaykhs.
For less than a quarter, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf give a choice among different modes of prayer, with preferences: pray in the garment (best), pray naked sitting with gestures (next), pray naked standing with bowing and prostration (last).
The commentary mentions a fourth option (gesturing while standing) but notes disagreement.
4
Analyze Imam Muhammad's objection.
Muhammad says no choice; you must pray in the garment because covering (satr) is obligatory and the impurity is overlooked due to necessity.
His reasoning: the command for purification fell due to inability, but the command for covering remains.
5
Compare the practical consequences of the two positions.
The difference affects whether you pray with some covering or completely naked. The two shaykhs allow both; Muhammad requires the garment.
Later Hanafi fatwa often favors the two shaykhs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد ثوباً ربعه طاهر وصلى عارياً لم يجز وخير إن طهر أقل من ربعه. قوله: (ولو وجد ثوباً ربعه طاهر وصلى عارياً لم يجر) لأن ربع الشيء يقوم مقام كله فيجعل كأن كله طاهر في موضع الضرورة فيفترض عليه الصلاة فيه. قوله (وخير إن طهر أقل من ربعه) يعني بين أن يصلي فيه وهو الأفضل لما فيه من الإتيان بالركوع والسجود وستر العورة، وبين أن يصلي عرياناً قاعداً يومئ بالركوع والسجود وهو يلي الأول في الفضل لما فيه من ستر العورة الغليظة، وبين أن قائماً عرياناً بركوع وسجود وهو دونهما في الفضل. وفي ملتقى البحار إن شاء صلى عرياناً بالركوع والسجود أو مومياً بهما إما قاعداً وإما قائماً فهذا نص على جواز الإيماء قائماً. وظاهر الهداية أنه لا يجوز. وعلى الأول المخير فيه أربعة أشياء، وينبغي أن يكون الرابع دون الثالث في الفضل وإن كان ستر العورة فيه أكثر للاختلاف في صحته. وهذا كله عندهما، وعند محمد ليس بمخير ولا تجوز صلاته إلا في الثوب لأن خطاب التطهير سقط عنه لعجزه ولم يسقط عنه خطاب الستر لقدرته عليه فصار كالطاهر في حقه.
If he finds a garment of which a quarter is pure and he prays naked, it is not valid, and he is given a choice if less than a quarter is pure. His statement: 'If he finds a garment of which a quarter is pure and he prays naked, it is not valid' because a quarter of a thing stands in place of the whole, so it is made as if the whole is pure in a situation of necessity, so it is obligatory to pray in it. His statement: 'And he is given a choice if less than a quarter is pure' means between praying in it, which is best due to performing bowing and prostration and covering the awrah, and praying naked while sitting gesturing with bowing and prostration, which is next in excellence due to covering the major awrah, and standing naked with bowing and prostration, which is below both in excellence. In Multaqa al-Bihar, if he wishes, he may pray naked with bowing and prostration or gesturing with them, either sitting or standing, so this text indicates the permissibility of gesturing while standing. The apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that it is not permissible. According to the first view, he is given a choice among four things, and it is proper that the fourth is lower in excellence than the third, even though covering the awrah is greater due to disagreement on its validity. All this according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), but according to Muhammad, he is not given a choice and his prayer is only valid in the garment, because the command for purification fell from him due to inability, but the command for covering did not fall due to his ability, so he becomes like a pure person in his regard.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a person who has a garment that is partially impure. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states two rulings: first, if the garment has a quarter that is pure, praying naked is invalid because the quarter has the legal effect of the whole in necessity; second, if less than a quarter is pure, the person has a choice. The commentary expands: the best option is to pray in the garment, then to pray naked sitting with gestures, then standing naked with bowing and prostration. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) grant this choice. However, Imam Muhammad holds that the person is not given a choice; he must pray in the garment because the obligation of covering remains binding. The text also notes a disagreement about gesturing while standing: some sources allow it, but al-Hidayah apparently does not. The student should note that this ruling applies only when the impurity is less than a quarter; if a quarter or more is pure, the garment must be worn. The dispute centers on whether the obligation of covering or the impurity takes precedence when the person is unable to purify.

Key Terms
ربع (rub'): Quarter of the garment; a legal measure determining whether the purity of part suffices for the whole.
تخيير (takhyīr): The right to choose between two valid options; here between praying in the impure garment or praying naked with gestures.
إيماء (īmā'): Gesturing with head or body instead of full bowing and prostration; permissible for the one praying naked when covering is minimal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if a person finds a garment with less than a quarter pure, what must he do?
A) He may choose to pray either in the garment or naked.
B) He must pray in the garment, even though it is impure.
C) He must pray naked by sitting with gestures.
D) He must pray in the garment only if it covers his awrah.

Key Takeaway
When less than a quarter of a garment is pure, the Hanafi school has a valid disagreement: the two shaykhs grant a choice, while Imam Muhammad requires wearing it.

Choosing the Lighter of Two Evils (Akhdh al-Akhaf min al-Baliyyatayn)
States a maxim with three fiqh applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of choosing the lesser evil when faced with two unavoidable harms, and apply it to cases of prayer and covering.

Core Idea
When forced between two harms, choose the lighter one; necessity overrides detail.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays down a key maxim that governs many cases of necessity (darurah) in Hanafi fiqh. When a person has no perfect option and must choose between two evils (baliyyatayn), the principle provides clear guidance: if the harms are equal, any choice is allowed; if one is lighter, that must be chosen. The text illustrates this with three concrete examples related to women's prayer, head covering, and a wounded person's prostration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two evils in a given scenario
Name both problematic options. For example, the woman's prayer: standing exposes awrah vs. sitting omits standing.
The evils are harms to valid prayer, not necessarily sins.
2
Compare the severity of the two evils
Determine if they are equal or one is lighter based on fiqh priorities. Exposing awrah invalidates prayer, while omitting standing is only wajib.
Hanafi school ranks obligations; standing is wajib, covering awrah is fard.
3
Apply the principle: choose the lighter, or any if equal
If one is lighter, that must be chosen. If equal, choice is free.
The lighter option causes less harm to the validity of the act.
4
Work through the examples in the text
For the woman: sitting (lighter) because it avoids awrah exposure. For the garment: covering less than quarter of head is excused; quarter or more requires full coverage. For the wounded: likely omit prostration to prevent bleeding.
The principle overrides the default detailed rulings when choice is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا علم أن التفصيل المتقدم إنما هو عند الاختيار، أما عند الضرورة فلا تفصيل. ثم الأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أن من ابتلي ببليتين وهما متساويتان يأخذ بأيهما شاء، وإن اختلفا فعليه أن يختار أهونهما. ولهذا لو أن امرأة لو صلت قائمة ينكشف من عورتها ما يمنع جواز الصلاة، ولو صلت قاعدة لا ينكشف منها شيء فإنها تصلي قاعدة لما أن ترك القيام أهون. ولو كان الثوب يغطي جسدها وربع رأسها فتركت تغطية الرأس لا يجوز ولو كان يغطي أقل من الربع لا يضر والستر أفضل تقليلاً للانكشاف. ولو كان جريح لو سجد سال جرحه وإن لم يسجد لم
And by this it is known that the detailed rulings above are only when there is a choice, but in case of necessity there is no detail. Then the principle in this category of issues is: whoever is tried with two evils that are equal may take whichever he wishes, and if they differ, he must choose the lighter of them. Hence, if a woman if she prays standing, her awrah becomes exposed in a way that prevents the validity of prayer, and if she prays sitting, nothing is exposed, then she prays sitting because leaving the standing is lighter. And if the garment covers her body and a quarter of her head, and she leaves covering the head, it is not permissible. But if it covers less than a quarter, it does not harm, and covering is better to minimize exposure. And if there is a wounded person, if he prostrates his wound bleeds, and if he does not prostrate it does not...
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage establishes a key qa'idah (legal maxim) in Hanafi fiqh: when a person is forced to choose between two bad options (baliyyatayn), if they are equal, he may choose either; if one is lighter, he must choose the lighter one. The text applies this to three examples. First, a woman whose awrah (the area that must be covered in prayer) is exposed when standing but not when sitting: she must pray sitting because leaving standing is the lighter evil. Second, if a garment covers the body and a quarter of the head, failing to cover the head invalidates prayer (since covering the head is wajib for women, and a quarter is the threshold), but if it covers less than a quarter, it is excused. Third, a wounded person whose wound would bleed if he prostrates but not if he does not: the principle would apply (the text cuts off). The student should understand that this principle is not just for prayer but applies across fiqh whenever two obligations conflict or when harm is unavoidable. The 'detail' referred to is the earlier rulings on the impure garment, which apply only when there is a choice (ikhtiyar), not when forced (darurah).

Key Terms
أصل (aṣl): A foundational principle or maxim governing a category of cases.
بليتان (baliyyatān): Two trials or harms that a person must choose between; both are problematic.
أهون (ahwan): Lighter, lesser evil; the option that causes less harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle stated, if a woman's awrah is exposed when standing but not when sitting, what should she do?
A) She may choose to pray either standing or sitting.
B) She must pray sitting.
C) She must pray standing despite the exposure.
D) She should delay the prayer until she can cover properly.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'choose the lighter of two evils' is a core Hanafi tool for resolving fiqh conflicts under necessity.

The Ruling for the Naked Person's Prayer: Sitting with Gestures
Explains the ruling and proof for naked prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the prayer of a person lacking clothing and cite the proof from the incident of the Prophet's companions.

Core Idea
The naked person prays sitting with gestures; covering the awrah takes priority over full postures.

In this passage, al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the basic ruling for one who has no garment to cover his awrah. The base text (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that such a person must pray sitting and gesture for bowing and prostration. The commentator supports this with the practice of the Prophet's companions when they were shipwrecked. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the hierarchy of obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is salah for a person without covering. The ruling: pray sitting with gestures (īmāʾ).
2
Locate the dalil
The incident of the Prophet's companions on the ship (narrated by Anas) is cited as evidence that they prayed sitting with gestures when naked.
The hadith is considered reliable in practice though not explicitly authenticated in the text.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The companions chose to sit and gesture to minimize exposure, showing that covering the awrah is prioritized over performing full postures.
4
Identify the preferred hierarchy
Sitting with gestures is explicitly stated as better (afḍal) than standing with full bowing and prostration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلاة قاعداً مومياً بركوع وسجود وهو أفضل من القيام بركوع وسجود والنية يسل فإنه يصلي قاعداً مومياً لأن ترك السجود أهون من الصلاة الحدث، ألا ترى أن ترك السجود جائز حالة الاختيار في التطوع على الدابة، ومع الحدث لا يجوز بحال. فإن قام وقرأ وركع ثم قعد وأومأ للسجود جاز لما قلنا والأول أفضل، وكذا شيخ لا يقدر على القراءة قائماً ويقدر عليها قاعداً يصلي قاعداً لأنه يجوز حالة الاختيار في النفل ولا يجوز ترك القراءة بحال، ولو صلى في الفصلين قائماً مع الحدث وترك القراءة لم يجز . قوله : ( ولو عدم ثوباً صلى قاعداً مومياً بركوع وسجود وهو أفضل من القيام بركوع وسجود) لما عن أنس أن أصحاب رسول الله وركبوا في السفينة فانكسرت بهم فخرجوا من البحر عراة فصلوا قعوداً بإيماء. أراد بالثوب ما يستر عامة عورته ولو حريراً أو حشيشاً أو نباتاً أو كلاً أو طيناً يلطخ به عورته ويبقى عليه حتى يصلي لا الزجاج الذي يصف ما تحته. والعدم المذكور يثبت بعدم الوجود في ملكه وبعدم الإباحة له حتى لو أبيح له ثوب تثبت القدرة به على الأصح ، فلو صلى عارياً لم يجز كالمتيمم إذا أبيح له الماء. وعن محمد في العريان يعده صاحبه أنه يعطيه الثوب إذا صلى فإنه ينتظره ولا يصلي عرياناً وإن خاف فوت الوقت. كذا في السراج الوهاج وفي القنية عن أبي حنيفة : ينتظره ما لم يخف فوت الوقت وأبو يوسف مع أبي حنيفة. وينبغي ترجيحه قياساً على المتيمم إذا كان يرجو الماء في آخره. في الصلاة قاعداً فشمل ما إذا كان نهاراً أو ليلاً في بيت أو صحراء وهو الصحيح كما بينه في منية المصلي، ومن المشايخ من خصه بالنهار، أما في الليل فيصلي قائماً لأن ظلمة الليل تستر عورته قال في الذخيرة وهذا ليس بمرضي لأن الستر الذي يحصل في ظلمة الليل لا عبرة به ألا ترى أن حالة القدرة على الثوب إذا صلى عرياناً في ظلمة الليل لا يجوز فصار وجوده وعدمه بمنزلة واحدة ا هـ. وتعقبه في شرح منية المصلي بأن الاستشهاد المذكور غير متجه للفرق بين حالة الاختيار وحالة الاضطرار وأطال إلى أن قال : ويؤيده ما أخرجه عبد الرزاق سئل علي رضي الله عنه عن صلاة العريان قال: إن كان حيث يراه الناس صلى جالساً، وإن كان حيث لا يراه الناس صلى قائماً. وهو وإن كان سنده ضعيفاً فلا يقصر عن إفادة الاستئناس وأما واقعة الصحابة المتقدمة فقد تطرق إليها احتمالات، إما لأنهم اختاروا الأولى لما فيه من تقليل الانكشاف أو لأنهم كانوا مترائين أو لم يكن ليلاً فسقط بها الاستدلال، ولم يبين المصنف صفة القعود للاختلاف فيها. ففي منية المصلي : يقعد كما يقعد في الصلاة. فعلى هذا يختلف في الرجل والمرأة، فهو يفترش وهي تتورك. وفي الذخيرة: يقعد ويمد وأطلق
And prayer sitting with gestures for bowing and prostration, and it is better than standing with bowing and prostration, and the intention... he prays sitting with gestures because leaving prostration is easier than praying with ritual impurity. Do you not see that leaving prostration is permissible in the state of choice in supererogatory prayers on a mount, but with ritual impurity it is not permissible under any circumstance. If he stood, recited, bowed, then sat down and gestured for prostration, it is permissible according to what we said, and the first is better. Similarly, an old man who cannot recite while standing but can while sitting, prays sitting because it is permissible in the state of choice in supererogatory prayers, and leaving recitation is never permissible. If he prays in both phases standing with impurity and leaving recitation, it is not permissible. His statement: (If he lacks a garment, he prays sitting with gestures for bowing and prostration, and it is better than standing with bowing and prostration) because of what is narrated from Anas that the companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) rode on a ship, and it broke with them, so they came out of the sea naked and prayed sitting with gestures. By 'garment' is meant what covers most of his awrah, even if it be silk, grass, plants, leaves, or mud with which he smears his awrah and remains on it until he prays, but not glass that reveals what is under it. The mentioned lack (of a garment) is established by the non-existence in his possession and by the non-permissibility for him; even if a garment is made permissible for him, ability is established with it according to the most correct view, so if he prays naked it is not valid, like the one performing tayammum when water is made permissible for him. According to Muhammad, regarding the naked person whose companion promises to give him a garment, he waits for him and does not pray naked even if he fears the time will pass. Likewise in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and in al-Qunyah from Abu Hanifah: He waits for him as long as he does not fear the time passing, and Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifah. And it is preferable to give preponderance to this view by analogy with the one performing tayammum if he hopes for water at the end of the time. His prayer sitting includes whether it is day or night, inside a house or in the desert, and this is the correct view as explained in Munyat al-Musalli. Some mashayikh restricted it to daytime, while at night he prays standing because the darkness of the night covers his awrah. Al-Dhakhirah said: This is not satisfactory because the covering obtained in the darkness of the night is not considered; do you not see that in the state of ability to have a garment, if he prays naked at night, it is not valid, so its existence and non-existence are alike. It was objected to in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli that the mentioned analogy is not appropriate for the distinction between the state of choice and the state of necessity, and he extended the discussion until he said: It is supported by what Abd al-Razzaq narrated: Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was asked about the prayer of the naked man; he said: If he is where people see him, he prays sitting; if he is where people do not see him, he prays standing. Even though its chain is weak, it is not less than providing comfort. As for the earlier incident of the companions, possibilities have been raised about it: either they chose the best because it minimizes exposure, or because they were visible to each other, or because it was not night, so the inference falls. The author did not specify the manner of sitting due to disagreement about it. In Munyat al-Musalli: He sits as he sits in prayer. On this basis, it differs for a man and a woman: he sits with iftirash, and she sits with tawarruk. In al-Dhakhirah: He sits and extends (his legs) and it is absolute.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the ruling for a person who has no garment to cover his awrah (al-ʿuryān). The matn (base text) states that such a person prays sitting with gestures (īmāʾ) for bowing and prostrating, and this is superior to standing with full bowing and prostration. The commentator expands with proof from the incident of the Prophet's companions who were shipwrecked and prayed naked while sitting. The reasoning given is that leaving prostration (by sitting) is a lesser loss than praying with ritual impurity (hadath), which is never allowed. The passage then defines what counts as a 'garment': anything that covers most of the awrah, even silk, grass, or mud, but not transparent material like glass. The loss of a garment can be due to lack of possession or lack of permission to use; if a garment is made permissible (e.g., someone offers to lend), the person is considered able and must pray clothed. A key intra-Hanafi dispute is whether one should wait for a promised garment: according to Muhammad (Ibn al-Hasan), one must wait even if the time may pass; according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, one waits only if there is no fear of time expiring. The commentator preponderates Muhammad's view by analogy with tayammum where one hopes for water. Another dispute concerns night-time darkness: some scholars say one may stand at night because darkness hides the awrah, but this is rejected because darkness does not actually cover; the ruling should be the same as daytime. The passage ends with a difference in how to sit: whether as in regular prayer (with iftirash for men, tawarruk for women) or simply extending the legs, and the author did not specify due to the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
العريان (al-ʿuryān): The person who has no garment to cover his awrah, whether due to lack of possession or lack of permission.
الإيماء (al-īmāʾ): Gestures substituting for bowing and prostration when one cannot perform them due to inability or, as here, due to lack of covering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a person who has no garment to cover his awrah?
A) He must pray standing with full movements.
B) He must pray sitting with gestures.
C) He is exempt from prayer until he finds a garment.
D) He may choose any posture.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates sitting with gestures for the naked person to preserve modesty, based on the practice of the Prophet's companions.
