The Hadith of the Musi' Salatahu and Its Use in Determining Fard Acts of Prayer
Argues that the hadith establishes fard acts and discusses evidential weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists use the hadith of the musu' to derive the fard acts of prayer and respond to the objection that a khabar wahid cannot establish obligation.

Core Idea
A khabar wahid with definitive dalalah confirms fard when context is teaching, even if thubut is zanni.

This lesson examines a classic proof (dalil) used to identify the obligatory (fard) acts of prayer: the hadith of the man who prayed improperly (musi' salatahu). Hanafi jurists argue that the Prophet's teaching to him lists the essential elements, implying that anything omitted is not fard. The passage then tackles a key methodological objection: since this hadith is a khabar wahid (single narration), can it really establish an obligation? We will see how the Hanafi school navigates transmission and indication to support its ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its transmission
Locate the full hadith as narrated by Abu Hurairah in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim. Note that the Prophet repeats the teaching after the man's failed prayer attempts.
The hadith's thubut (transmission) is zanni because it is a khabar wahid.
2
Understand the Hanafi use of the hadith as dalil
Hanafi jurists cite this hadith to prove that the acts mentioned (e.g., takbir, recitation, bowing, prostration, tranquility) are fard, and acts not mentioned (e.g., opening supplication, tashahhud, salam) are not fard.
The argument hinges on the context of teaching and the principle that delaying clarification is impermissible.
3
Analyze the reasoning for obligation of mentioned acts
The command form (if'al) in the hadith indicates obligation in Hanafi usul. Each commanded act—ablution, facing qibla, takbir, recitation, bowing, prostration, and tranquility—is therefore fard.
This is a direct application of 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub'.
4
Analyze the reasoning for non-obligation of unmentioned acts
Because the Prophet was teaching the prayer, his omission of certain acts (like du'a al-istiftah) implies they are not obligatory. Delaying explanation would violate the principle that clarification must be timely.
This is not mafhum al-mukhalafah but implicit from the context of a teaching moment.
5
Evaluate the objection regarding khabar wahid
Some critics argue that a khabar wahid cannot establish a fard. The Hanafi response: though transmission is zanni, the dalalah (indication) of the hadith is qat'i because the teaching context makes the meaning definitive. Such hadith are used to confirm obligations already indicated by other evidence.
This reflects the Hanafi distinction between thubut and dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وعليك السلام ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل حتى فعل ذلك ثلاث مرات فقال الرجل : والذي بعثك بالحق ما أحسن غير هذا فعلمني قال : إذا قمت إلى الصلاة فأسبغ الوضوء ثم استقبل القبلة فكبر، ثم اقرأ ما تيسر قائماً، معك من القرآن، ثم اركع حتى تطمئن راكعاً، ثم ارفع حتى تستوي اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجداً، ثم ارفع حتى تطمئن جالساً، ثم اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجداً ثم ارفع حتى تستوي قائماً ، ثم افعل ذلك في صلاتك كلها استدل الفقهاء بهذا الحديث بالوضوء واستقبال القبلة والتكبير وقراءة القرآن بما تيسر والركوع والرفع منه والسجدة الأولى والرفع منها والثانية والرفع منها، فيدل الأمر على وجوب هذه الأشياء. وقوله حتى تطمئن راكعاً . وحتى تطمئن وحتى تطمئن جالساً وحتى تستوي قائماً يدل على وجوب تعديل الأركان فيها. هذا ما ذكر في الحديث. وأما استدلالهم على عدم وجوب ما لم يذكر فيه فمنه ما استدلوا على عدم وجوب دعاء الاستفتاح لأنه لم يذكر فيه ومنه ما استدل بعض المالكية على عدم وجوب التشهد لذلك، ومنه استدل بعض الحنفية على عدم وجوب السلام لذلك وقد كثر كلام الفقهاء فيه طرداً وعكساً. وقال بعض الشارحين : رداً لاستدلالهم والحق أن هذا خبر واحد لا يفيد المواضع في كتبهم لإثبات فرضية شيء أنه فرض بالنقل والعقل،
His statement: (and by the Sunnah). Then and its glosses, connected to his statement 'by the Book'. His statement: 'When you stand for prayer, then perform ablution perfectly...' and the complete hadith is what is mentioned in the two Sahihs with their chain to Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: A man entered the mosque while the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was sitting in a corner of the mosque. He prayed, then came and greeted the Prophet, peace be upon him. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'And upon you be peace. Go back and pray, for you have not prayed.' So he went back and prayed as he had prayed, then came and greeted him. He said: 'And upon you be peace. Go back and pray, for you have not prayed.' He did this three times. Then the man said: 'By Him who sent you with the truth, I cannot do better than this; teach me.' He said: 'When you stand for prayer, perform ablution perfectly, then face the qibla and say takbir. Then recite what is easy for you of the Quran while standing. Then bow until you are at ease in bowing. Then rise until you are standing straight. Then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration. Then rise until you are at ease sitting. Then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration. Then rise until you are standing straight. Then do that in all your prayers.' The jurists used this hadith to deduce the obligation (fardiyyah) of what is mentioned in it, whether it is done inside or outside the prayer, and the non-obligation of what is not mentioned in it in the prayer. As for the obligation of what is mentioned in it, it is because it is commanded, and a command indicates obligation as known in usul. As for the non-obligation of what is not mentioned in it in the prayer, it is because the context is a context of teaching prayer and defining its pillars, and that requires confining the obligations to what is mentioned in it, lest there be a delay in clarification at the time of need, which is not permissible. The detail is that he, peace be upon him, commanded him in this hadith to perform ablution, face the qibla, say takbir, recite the Quran as much as is easy, bow, rise from it, the first prostration, rise from it, the second prostration, and rise from it. So the command indicates the obligation of these things. His statement 'until you are at ease in bowing' and 'until you are at ease' and 'until you are at ease sitting' and 'until you are standing straight' indicates the obligation of performing the pillars properly. This is what is mentioned in the hadith. As for their deduction of the non-obligation of what is not mentioned in it, part of it is their deduction of the non-obligation of the opening supplication because it is not mentioned in it, and part of it is the deduction of some Malikis of the non-obligation of the tashahhud for that reason, and part of it is the deduction of some Hanafis of the non-obligation of the taslim for that reason. The jurists have discussed it extensively, positively and negatively. Some commentators said, refuting their deduction, 'The truth is that this is a single narration (khabar wahid) that does not establish the obligation of anything at all.' I say: Their deduction is correct. As for the view of al-Shafi'i and Malik, it is apparent because they consider that an obligation can be established by a khabar wahid. As for our madhhab, it is also like that because such a deduction—I mean the deduction by the very concept of a non-definitive text to establish the obligation of something if its indication is definitive—is common and frequent among scholars, even if it is not independent in establishing it due to the non-definitiveness of its transmission. They intend by that to confirm the content of what is definitive. Do you not see that they say in many places in their books to establish the obligation of something that it is obligatory by transmission and reason?
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq explaining the use of the hadith of the musu' (the man who prayed poorly) to determine the obligatory (fard) acts of prayer. The author first presents the full hadith from the Sahihayn, where the Prophet taught a man the essentials of prayer. The jurists, including Hanafis, use this hadith to argue that anything mentioned in it (like wudu, facing qibla, takbir, recitation, bowing, prostration, and tranquility in each posture) is obligatory, and anything not mentioned (like the opening supplication, tashahhud, or salam) is not obligatory according to some scholars. The passage then addresses an objection: that this hadith is a khabar wahid (single narration) which, in the Hanafi school, does not establish obligation by itself. The commentator argues that the deduction is still valid because the hadith's indication (dalalah) is definitive—it is a teaching context—and scholars use it to confirm obligations that are already established by other evidence. This shows the Hanafi method of combining textual evidence with rational principles.

Key Terms
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A tradition transmitted by a single chain or a few individuals, which is zanni al-thubut (probable of transmission).
فرض (fard): An obligation established by a definitive proof (qat'i), such as the Quran or mutawatir Sunnah; or in this context, established by a clear command even from ahad hadith when supported.
دلالة (dalalah): The indication or meaning derived from a text; here, the hadith's dalalah is considered qat'i (definitive) because it is a teaching context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do Hanafi jurists accept the hadith of the musu' as evidence for the fard acts of prayer despite it being a khabar wahid?
A) Because the hadith is mutawatir.
B) Because the hadith's indication is definitive (dalalah qat'iyyah) and the context is teaching, so it confirms obligations even if transmission is not definitive.
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa personally authenticated the hadith.
D) Because the hadith is from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
A khabar wahid with definitive dalalah can confirm fard acts, but only in conjunction with other evidence; the hadith of the musu' is a classic example of this Hanafi method.
